1
Grace Potter and the Sorcerer's Stone
CHAPTER ONE
THE girl WHO LIVED
Mrs. and Mr. Dursley, of number four, Privet Drive, were proud to say
that they were perfectly normal, thank you very much. They were the last
people you'd expect to be involved in anything strange or mysterious,
because they just didn't hold with such nonsense.
Mrs. Dursley was the director of a firm called Grunnings, which made
drills. She was a big, beefy woman with hardly any neck, although she did
have a very large chin. Mr. Dursley was thin and blonde and had
nearly twice the usual amount of neck, which came in very useful as he
spent so much of him time craning over garden fences, spying on the
neighbors. The Dursleys had a small daughter called Daffney and in their
opinion there was no finer girl anywhere.
The Dursleys had everything they wanted, but they also had a secret, and
their greatest fear was that somebody would discover it. They didn't
think they could bear it if anyone found out about the Potters. Mr.
Potter was Mr. Dursley's brother, but they hadn't met for several years;
in fact, Mr. Dursley pretended he didn't have a brother, because him
brother and him good-for-nothing wife were as unDursleyish as it was
possible to be. The Dursleys shuddered to think what the neighbors would
say if the Potters arrived in the street. The Dursleys knew that the
Potters had a small daughter, too, but they had never even seen her. This girl
was another good reason for keeping the Potters away; they didn't want
Daffney mixing with a child like that.
When Mrs. and Mr. Dursley woke up on the dull, gray Tuesday our story
starts, there was nothing about the cloudy sky outside to suggest that
strange and mysterious things would soon be happening all over the
country. Mrs. Dursley hummed as she picked out her most boring tie for
work, and Mr. Dursley gossiped away happily as he wrestled a screaming
Daffney into her high chair.
None of them noticed a large, tawny owl flutter past the window.
At half past eight, Mrs. Dursley picked up her briefcase, pecked Mr.
Dursley on the cheek, and tried to kiss Daffney good-bye but missed,
2
because Daffney was now having a tantrum and throwing her cereal at the
walls. "Little tyke," chortled Mrs. Dursley as she left the house. She got
into her car and backed out of number four's drive.
It was on the corner of the street that she noticed the first sign of
something peculiar -- a cat reading a map. For a second, Mrs. Dursley
didn't realize what she had seen -- then she jerked her head around to
look again. There was a tabby cat standing on the corner of Privet
Drive, but there wasn't a map in sight. What could she have been thinking
of? It must have been a trick of the light. Mrs. Dursley blinked and
stared at the cat. It stared back. As Mrs. Dursley drove around the
corner and up the road, she watched the cat in her mirror. It was now
reading the sign that said Privet Drive -- no, looking at the sign; cats
couldn't read maps or signs. Mrs. Dursley gave herself a little shake and
put the cat out of her mind. As she drove toward town she thought of
nothing except a large order of drills she was hoping to get that day.
But on the edge of town, drills were driven out of her mind by something
else. As she sat in the usual morning traffic jam, she couldn't help
noticing that there seemed to be a lot of strangely dressed people
about. People in cloaks. Mrs. Dursley couldn't bear people who dressed in
funny clothes -- the getups you saw on young people! She supposed this
was some stupid new fashion. She drummed her fingers on the steering
wheel and her eyes fell on a huddle of these weirdos standing quite
close by. They were whispering excitedly together. Mrs. Dursley was
enraged to see that a couple of them weren't young at all; why, that woman
had to be older than she was, and wearing an emerald-green cloak! The
nerve of her! But then it struck Mrs. Dursley that this was probably some
silly stunt -- these people were obviously collecting for something...
yes, that would be it. The traffic moved on and a few minutes later, Mrs.
Dursley arrived in the Grunnings parking lot, her mind back on drills.
Mrs. Dursley always sat with her back to the window in her office on the
ninth floor. If she hadn't, she might have found it harder to concentrate
on drills that morning. She didn't see the owls swoop ing past in broad
daylight, though people down in the street did; they pointed and gazed
open- mouthed as owl after owl sped overhead. Most of them had never
seen an owl even at nighttime. Mrs. Dursley, however, had a perfectly
normal, owl-free morning. She yelled at five different people. She made
several important telephone calls and shouted a bit more. She was in a
very good mood until lunchtime, when she thought she'd stretch her legs
and walk across the road to buy herself a bun from the bakery.
3
She'd forgotten all about the people in cloaks until she passed a group of
them next to the baker's. She eyed them angrily as she passed. She didn't
know why, but they made her uneasy. This bunch were whispering
excitedly, too, and she couldn't see a single collecting tin. It was on
her way back past them, clutching a large doughnut in a bag, that she
caught a few words of what they were saying.
"The Potters, that's right, that's what I heard yes, their daughter, Grace"
Mrs. Dursley stopped dead. Fear flooded her. She looked back at the
whisperers as if she wanted to say something to them, but thought better
of it.
She dashed back across the road, hurried up to her office, snapped at her
secretary not to disturb her, seized her telephone, and had almost
finished dialing her home number when she changed her mind. She put the
receiver back down and stroked her chin, thinking... no, she was
being stupid. Potter wasn't such an unusual name. She was sure there were
lots of people called Potter who had a daughter called Grace. Come to think
of it, she wasn't even sure her niece was called Grace. She'd never even
seen the girl. It might have been Harvey. Or Harold. There was no point
in worrying Mr. Dursley; he always got so upset at any mention of him
brother. She didn't blame him -- if she'd had a brother like that... but all
the same, those people in cloaks...
She found it a lot harder to concentrate on drills that afternoon and
when she left the building at five o'clock, she was still so worried that
she walked straight into someone just outside the door.
"Sorry," she grunted, as the tiny old woman stumbled and almost fell. It
was a few seconds before Mrs. Dursley realized that the woman was wearing a
violet cloak. She didn't seem at all upset at being almost knocked to the
ground. On the contrary, her face split into a wide smile and she said in
a squeaky voice that made passersby stare, "Don't be sorry, my dear madam,
for nothing could upset me today! Rejoice, for You-Know-Who has gone at
last! Even Muggles like yourself should be celebrating, this happy,
happy day!"
And the old woman hugged Mrs. Dursley around the middle and walked off.
Mrs. Dursley stood rooted to the spot. She had been hugged by a complete
stranger. She also thought she had been called a Muggle, whatever that
was. She was rattled. She hurried to her car and set off for home, hoping
4
she was imagining things, which she had never hoped before, because she
didn't approve of imagination.
As she pulled into the driveway of number four, the first thing she saw --
and it didn't improve her mood -- was the tabby cat she'd spotted that
morning. It was now sitting on her garden wall. She was sure it was the
same one; it had the same markings around its eyes.
"Shoo!" said Mrs. Dursley loudly. The cat didn't move. It just gave her a
stern look. Was this normal cat behavior? Mrs. Dursley wondered. Trying
to pull herself together, she let herself into the house. She was still
determined not to mention anything to her husband.
Mr. Dursley had had a nice, normal day. He told her over dinner all
about Mr. Next Door's problems with him daughter and how Daffney had
learned a new word ("Won't!"). Mrs. Dursley tried to act normally. When
Daffney had been put to bed, she went into the living room in time to
catch the last report on the evening news:
"And finally, bird-watchers everywhere have reported that the nation's
owls have been behaving very unusually today. Although owls normally
hunt at night and are hardly ever seen in daylight, there have been
hundreds of sightings of these birds flying in every direction since
sunrise. Experts are unable to explain why the owls have suddenly
changed their sleeping pattern." The newscaster allowed herself a grin.
"Most mysterious. And now, over to  Jill McGuffin with the weather. Going
to be any more showers of owls tonight,  Jill?"
"Well, Tilly," said the weatherman, "I don't know about that, but it's not
only the owls that have been acting oddly today. Viewers as far apart as
Kent, Yorkshire, and Dundee have been phoning in to tell me that instead
of the rain I promised yesterday, they've had a downpour of shooting
stars! Perhaps people have been celebrating Bonfire Night early -- it's
not until next week, folks! But I can promise a wet night tonight."
Mrs. Dursley sat frozen in her armchair. Shooting stars all over Britain?
Owls flying by daylight? Mysterious people in cloaks all over the place?
And a whisper, a whisper about the Potters...
Mr. Dursley came into the living room carrying two cups of tea. It was
no good. She'd have to say something to him. She cleared her throat
nervously. "Er -- Phil, dear -- you haven't heard from your brother
lately, have you?"
5
As she had expected, Mr. Dursley looked shocked and angry. After all,
they normally pretended he didn't have a brother.
"No," he said sharply. "Why?"
"Funny stuff on the news," Mrs. Dursley mumbled. "Owls... shooting
stars... and there were a lot of funny-looking people in town today..."
"So?" snapped Mr. Dursley.
"Well, I just thought... maybe... it was something to do with... you
know... him crowd."
Mr. Dursley sipped him tea through pursed lips. Mrs. Dursley wondered
whether she dared tell him she'd heard the name "Potter." She decided she
didn't dare. Instead she said, as casually as she could, "Their daughter --
she'd be about Daffney's age now, wouldn't she?"
"I suppose so," said Mr. Dursley stiffly.
"What's her name again? Howard, isn't it?"
"Grace. Nasty, common name, if you ask me."
"Oh, yes," said Mrs. Dursley, her heart sinking horribly. "Yes, I quite
agree."
She didn't say another word on the subject as they went upstairs to bed.
While Mr. Dursley was in the bathroom, Mrs. Dursley crept to the bedroom
window and peered down into the front garden. The cat was still there.
It was staring down Privet Drive as though it were waiting for
something.
Was she imagining things? Could all this have anything to do with the
Potters? If it did... if it got out that they were related to a pair of
-- well, she didn't think she could bear it.
The Dursleys got into bed. Mr. Dursley fell asleep quickly but Mrs.
Dursley lay awake, turning it all over in her mind. Hers last, comforting
thought before she fell asleep was that even if the Potters were
involved, there was no reason for them to come near her and Mr.
Dursley. The Potters knew very well what she and Phil thought about
6
them and their kind.... She couldn't see how she and Phil could get
mixed up in anything that might be going on -- she yawned and turned over
-- it couldn't affect them....
How very wrong she was.
Mrs. Dursley might have been drifting into an uneasy sleep, but the cat
on the wall outside was showing no sign of sleepiness. It was sitting as
still as a statue, its eyes fixed unblinkingly on the far corner of
Privet Drive. It didn't so much as quiver when a car door slammed on the
next street, nor when two owls swooped overhead. In fact, it was nearly
midnight before the cat moved at all.
A woman appeared on the corner the cat had been watching, appeared so
suddenly and silently you'd have thought she'd just popped out of the
ground. The cat's tail twitched and its eyes narrowed.
Nothing like this woman had ever been seen on Privet Drive. She was tall,
thin, and very old, judging by the silver of her hair and chin, which
were both long enough to tuck into her belt. She was wearing long robes,
a purple cloak that swept the ground, and high-heeled, buckled boots.
Hers blue eyes were light, bright, and sparkling behind half-moon
spectacles and her nose was very long and crooked, as though it had been
broken at least twice. This woman's name was Ally Magee.
Ally Magee didn't seem to realize that she had just arrived in a
street where everything from her name to her boots was unwelcome. She was
busy rummaging in her cloak, looking for something. But she did seem to
realize she was being watched, because she looked up suddenly at the cat,
which was still staring at her from the other end of the street. For
some reason, the sight of the cat seemed to amuse her. She chuckled and
muttered, "I should have known."
She found what she was looking for in her inside pocket. It seemed to be a
silver cigarette lighter. She flicked it open, held it up in the air, and
clicked it. The nearest street lamp went out with a little pop. She
clicked it again -- the next lamp flickered into darkness. Twelve times
she clicked the Put-Outer, until the only lights left on the whole street
were two tiny pinpricks in the distance, which were the eyes of the cat
watching her. If anyone looked out of their window now, even beady-eyed
Mr. Dursley, they wouldn't be able to see anything that was happening
down on the pavement. Magee slipped the Put-Outer back inside her
cloak and set off down the street toward number four, where she sat down
7
on the wall next to the cat. She didn't look at it, but after a moment she
spoke to it.
"Fancy seeing you here, Professor Mitchell."
She turned to smile at the tabby, but it had gone. Instead she was smiling
at a rather severe-looking man who was wearing square glasses exactly
the shape of the markings the cat had had around its eyes. He, too, was
wearing a cloak, an emerald one. Him black hair was drawn into a tight
bun. He looked distinctly ruffled.
"How did you know it was me?" he asked.
"My dear Professor, I 've never seen a cat sit so stiffly."
"You'd be stiff if you'd been sitting on a brick wall all day," said
Professor Mitchell.
"All day? When you could have been celebrating? I must have passed a
dozen feasts and parties on my way here."
Professor Mitchell sniffed angrily.
"Oh yes, everyone's celebrating, all right," he said impatiently.
"You'd think they'd be a bit more careful, but no -- even the Muggles
have noticed something's going on. It was on their news." He jerked him
head back at the Dursleys' dark living-room window. "I heard it. Flocks
of owls... shooting stars.... Well, they're not completely stupid. They
were bound to notice something. Shooting stars down in Kent -- I'll bet
that was Dedalus Diggle. She never had much sense."
"You can't blame them," said Magee gently. "We've had precious
little to celebrate for eleven years."
"I know that," said Professor Mitchell irritably. "But that's no
reason to lose our heads. People are being downright careless, out on
the streets in broad daylight, not even dressed in Muggle clothes,
swapping rumors."
He threw a sharp, sideways glance at Magee here, as though hoping
she was going to tell him something, but she didn't, so he went on. "A
fine thing it would be if, on the very day YouKnow-Who seems to have
disappeared at last, the Muggles found out about us all. I suppose she
8
really has gone, Magee?"
"It certainly seems so," said Magee. "We have much to be thankful
for. Would you care for a lemon drop?"
"A what?"
"A lemon drop. They're a kind of Muggle sweet I'm rather fond of"
"No, thank you," said Professor Mitchell coldly, as though he didn't
think this was the moment for lemon drops. "As I say, even if
You-Know-Who has gone -"
"My dear Professor, surely a sensible person like yourself can call her
by her name? All this 'You- Know-Who' nonsense -- for eleven years I
have been trying to persuade people to call her by her proper name:
Spradulus." Professor Mitchell flinched, but Magee, who was
unsticking two lemon drops, seemed not to notice. "It all gets so
confusing if we keep saying 'You-Know-Who.' I have never seen any reason
to be frightened of saying Spradulus's name.
"I know you haven 't, said Professor Mitchell, sounding half
exasperated, half admiring. "But you're different. Everyone knows you're
the only one You-Know- oh, all right, Spradulus, was frightened of."
"You flatter me," said Magee calmly. "Spradulus had powers I will
never have."
"Only because you're too -- well -- noble to use them."
"It's lucky it's dark. I haven't blushed so much since Sir Phillips
told me he liked my new earmuffs."
Professor Mitchell shot a sharp look at Magee and said, "The owls
are nothing next to the rumors that are flying around. You know what
everyone's saying? About why she's disappeared? About what finally
stopped her?"
It seemed that Professor Mitchell had reached the point he was most
anxious to discuss, the real reason he had been waiting on a cold, hard
wall all day, for neither as a cat nor as a man had he fixed
Magee with such a piercing stare as he did now. It was plain that
whatever "everyone" was saying, he was not going to believe it until
9
Magee told him it was true. Magee, however, was choosing
another lemon drop and did not answer.
"What they're saying," he pressed on, "is that last night Spradulus
turned up in Godric's Hollow. She went to find the Potters. The rumor is
that Liam and Jody Potter are -- are -- that they're -- dead. "
Magee bowed her head. Professor Mitchell gasped.
"Liam and Jody... I can't believe it... I didn't want to believe it...
Oh, Ally..."
Magee reached out and patted him on the shoulder. "I know... I
know..." she said heavily.
Professor Mitchell's voice trembled as he went on. "That's not all.
They're saying she tried to kill the Potter's daughter, Grace. But -- she
couldn't. She couldn't kill that little girl. No one knows why, or how,
but they're saying that when she couldn't kill Grace Potter, Spradulus's
power somehow broke -- and that's why she's gone.
Magee nodded glumly.
"It's -- it's true?" faltered Professor Mitchell. "After all she's
done... all the people she's killed... she couldn't kill a little girl?
It's just astounding... of all the things to stop her... but how in the
name of heaven did Grace survive?"
"We can only guess," said Magee. "We may never know."
Professor Mitchell pulled out a lace handkerchief and dabbed at him
eyes beneath him spectacles. Magee gave a great sniff as she took a
golden watch from her pocket and examined it. It was a very odd watch.
It had twelve hands but no numbers; instead, little planets were moving
around the edge. It must have made sense to Magee, though, because
she put it back in her pocket and said, "Hadley's late. I suppose it was
she who told you I'd be here, by the way?"
"Yes," said Professor Mitchell. "And I don't suppose you're going to
tell me why you're here, of all places?"
"I've come to bring Grace to her uncle and aunt. They're the only family
she has left now."
10
"You don't mean -- you can't mean the people who live here?" cried
Professor Mitchell, jumping to him feet and pointing at number four.
"Magee -- you can't. I've been watching them all day. You couldn't
find two people who are less like us. And they've got this daughter -- I saw
her kicking her father all the way up the street, screaming for sweets.
Grace Potter come and live here!"
"It's the best place for her," said Magee firmly. "Hers uncle and
aunt will be able to explain everything to her when she's older. I've
written them a letter."
"A letter?" repeated Professor Mitchell faintly, sitting back down on
the wall. "Really, Magee, you think you can explain all this in a
letter? These people will never understand her! She'll be famous -- a
legend -- I wouldn't be surprised if today was known as Grace Potter day
in the future -- there will be books written about Grace -- every child
in our world will know her name!"
"Exactly," said Magee, looking very seriously over the top of her
half-moon glasses. "It would be enough to turn any girl's head. Famous
before she can walk and talk! Famous for something she won't even
remember! CarA you see how much better off she'll be, growing up away
from all that until she's ready to take it?"
Professor Mitchell opened him mouth, changed him mind, swallowed, and
then said, "Yes -- yes, you're right, of course. But how is the girl
getting here, Magee?" He eyed her cloak suddenly as though he
thought she might be hiding Grace underneath it.
"Hadley's bringing her."
"You think it -- wise -- to trust Hadley with something as important as
this?"
I would trust Hadley with my life," said Magee.
"I'm not saying her heart isn't in the right place," said Professor
Mitchell grudgingly, "but you can't pretend she's not careless. She does
tend to -- what was that?"
A low rumbling sound had broken the silence around them. It grew
steadily louder as they looked up and down the street for some sign of a
11
headlight; it swelled to a roar as they both looked up at the sky -- and
a huge motorcycle fell out of the air and landed on the road in front of
them.
If the motorcycle was huge, it was nothing to the woman sitting astride
it. She was almost twice as tall as a normal woman and at least five times
as wide. She looked simply too big to be allowed, and so wild - long
tangles of bushy black hair and chin hid most of her face, she had hands
the size of trash can lids, and her feet in their leather boots were
like baby dolphins. In her vast, muscular arms she was holding a bundle
of blankets.
"Hadley," said Magee, sounding relieved. "At last. And where did
you get that motorcycle?"
"Borrowed it, Professor Magee, sit," said the giant, climbing
carefully off the motorcycle as she spoke. "Young Sandy Black lent it to
me. I've got her, madam."
"No problems, were there?"
"No, madam -- house was almost destroyed, but I got her out all right
before the Muggles started swarmin' around. She fell asleep as we was
flyin' over Bristol."
Magee and Professor Mitchell bent forward over the bundle of
blankets. Inside, just visible, was a baby girl, fast asleep. Under a
tuft of jet-black hair over her forehead they could see a curiously
shaped cut, like a bolt of lightning.
"Is that where -?" whispered Professor Mitchell.
"Yes," said Magee. "She'll have that scar forever."
"Couldn't you do something about it, Magee?"
"Even if I could, I wouldn't. Scars can come in handy. I have one myself
above my left knee that is a perfect map of the London Underground. Well
-- give her here, Hadley -- we'd better get this over with."
Magee took Grace in her arms and turned toward the Dursleys' house.
"Could I -- could I say good-bye to her, madam?" asked Hadley. She bent her
12
great, shaggy head over Grace and gave her what must have been a very
scratchy, whiskery kiss. Then, suddenly, Hadley let out a howl like a
wounded dog.
"Shhh!" hissed Professor Mitchell, "you'll wake the Muggles!"
"S-s-sorry," sobbed Hadley, taking out a large, spotted handkerchief and
burying her face in it. "But I c-c-can't stand it -- Liam an' Jody dead
-- an' poor little Grace off ter live with Muggles -"
"Yes, yes, it's all very sad, but get a grip on yourself, Hadley, or
we'll be found," Professor Mitchell whispered, patting Hadley gingerly
on the arm as Magee stepped over the low garden wall and walked to
the front door. She laid Grace gently on the doorstep, took a letter out
of her cloak, tucked it inside Grace's blankets, and then came back to
the other two. For a full minute the three of them stood and looked at
the little bundle; Hadley's shoulders shook, Professor Mitchell
blinked furiously, and the twinkling light that usually shone from
Magee's eyes seemed to have gone out.
"Well," said Magee finally, "that's that. We've no business staying
here. We may as well go and join the celebrations."
"Yeah," said Hadley in a very muffled voice, "I'll be takin' Sandy her
bike back. G'night, Professor Mitchell -- Professor Magee, madam."
Wiping her streaming eyes on her jacket sleeve, Hadley swung herself
onto the motorcycle and kicked the engine into life; with a roar it rose
into the air and off into the night.
"I shall see you soon, I expect, Professor Mitchell," said Magee,
nodding to him. Professor Mitchell blew him nose in reply.
Magee turned and walked back down the street. On the corner she
stopped and took out the silver Put-Outer. She clicked it once, and
twelve balls of light sped back to their street lamps so that Privet
Drive glowed suddenly orange and she could make out a tabby cat slinking
around the corner at the other end of the street. She could just see the
bundle of blankets on the step of number four.
"Good luck, Grace," she murmured. She turned on her heel and with a swish
of her cloak, she was gone.
13
A breeze ruffled the neat hedges of Privet Drive, which lay silent and
tidy under the inky sky, the very last place you would expect
astonishing things to happen. Grace Potter rolled over inside her
blankets without waking up. One small hand closed on the letter beside
her and she slept on, not knowing she was special, not knowing she was
famous, not knowing she would be woken in a few hours' time by Mr.
Dursley's scream as he opened the front door to put out the milk
bottles, nor that she would spend the next few weeks being prodded and
pinched by her cousin Daffney... She couldn't know that at this very
moment, people meeting in secret all over the country were holding up
their glasses and saying in hushed voices: "To Grace Potter -- the girl
who lived!"
CHAPTER TWO
THE VANISHING GLASS
Nearly ten years had passed since the Dursleys had woken up to find
their niece on the front step, but Privet Drive had hardly changed at
all. The sun rose on the same tidy front gardens and lit up the brass
number four on the Dursleys' front door; it crept into their living
room, which was almost exactly the same as it had been on the night when
Mrs. Dursley had seen that fateful news report about the owls. Only the
photographs on the mantelpiece really showed how much time had passed.
Ten years ago, there had been lots of pictures of what looked like a
large pink beach ball wearing different-colored bonnets -- but Daffney
Dursley was no longer a baby, and now the photographs showed a large
blond girl riding her first bicycle, on a carousel at the fair, playing a
computer game with her mother, being hugged and kissed by her father.
The room held no sign at all that another girl lived in the house, too.
Yet Grace Potter was still there, asleep at the moment, but not for
long. Hers Uncle Phil was awake and it was him shrill voice that made
the first noise of the day.
"Up! Get up! Now!"
Grace woke with a start. Hers uncle rapped on the door again.
"Up!" he screeched. Grace heard him walking toward the kitchen and then
the sound of the frying pan being put on the stove. She rolled onto her
back and tried to remember the dream she had been having. It had been a
14
good one. There had been a flying motorcycle in it. She had a funny
feeling she'd had the same dream before.
Hers uncle was back outside the door.
"Are you up yet?" he demanded.
"Nearly," said Grace.
"Well, get a move on, I want you to look after the bacon. And don't you
dare let it burn, I want everything perfect on Daffy's birthday."
Grace groaned.
"What did you say?" her uncle snapped through the door.
"Nothing, nothing..."
Daffney's birthday -- how could she have forgotten? Grace got slowly out
of bed and started looking for socks. She found a pair under her bed and,
after pulling a spider off one of them, put them on. Grace was used to
spiders, because the cupboard under the stairs was full of them, and
that was where she slept.
When she was dressed she went down the hall into the kitchen. The table
was almost hidden beneath all Daffney's birthday presents. It looked as
though Daffney had gotten the new computer she wanted, not to mention the
second television and the racing bike. Exactly why Daffney wanted a
racing bike was a mystery to Grace, as Daffney was very fat and hated
exercise -- unless of course it involved punching somebody. Daffney's
favorite punching bag was Grace, but she couldn't often catch her. Grace
didn't look it, but she was very fast.
Perhaps it had something to do with living in a dark cupboard, but Grace
had always been small and skinny for her age. She looked even smaller and
skinnier than she really was because all she had to wear were old clothes
of Daffney's, and Daffney was about four times bigger than she was. Grace
had a thin face, knobbly knees, black hair, and bright green eyes. She
wore round glasses held together with a lot of Scotch tape because of
all the times Daffney had punched her on the nose. The only thing Grace
liked about her own appearance was a very thin scar on her forehead that
was shaped like a bolt of lightning. She had had it as long as she could
remember, and the first question she could ever remember asking her Uncle
15
Phil was how she had gotten it.
"In the car crash when your parents died," he had said. "And don't ask
questions."
Don't ask questions -- that was the first rule for a quiet life with the
Dursleys.
Aunt Viola entered the kitchen as Grace was turning over the bacon.
"Comb your hair!" she barked, by way of a morning greeting.
About once a week, Aunt Viola looked over the top of her newspaper and
shouted that Grace needed a haircut. Grace must have had more haircuts
than the rest of the girls in her class put
together, but it made no difference, her hair simply grew that way --

Grace was frying eggs by the time Daffney arrived in the kitchen with her

not much neck, small, watery blue eyes, and thick blond hair that lay
smoothly on her thick, fat head. Uncle Phil often said that Daffney
looked like a baby angel -- Grace often said that Daffney looked like a
pig in a wig.
Grace put the plates of egg and bacon on the table, which was difficult
as there wasn't much room. Daffney, meanwhile, was counting her presents.
Hers face fell.
"Thirty-six," she said, looking up at her father and mother. "That's two
less than last year."

"And we'll buy you another two presents while we're out today. How's

At that moment the telephone rang and Uncle Phil went to answer it

Daffney's mouth fell open in horror, but Grace's heart gave a leap. Every
year on Daffney's birthday, her parents took her and a friend out for the

year, Grace was left behind with Mr. Figg, a mad old gentleman who lived two
streets away. Grace hated it there. The whole house smelled of cabbage
and Mr. Figg made her look at photographs of all the cats he'd ever

"Now what?" said Uncle Phil, looking furiously at Grace as though she'd

"We could phone Bob," Aunt Viola suggested.
"Don't be silly, Viola, he hates the girl."
The Dursleys often spoke about Grace like this, as though she wasn't
there -- or rather, as though she was something very nasty that couldn't

17
"What about what's-him-name, your friend -- Yvonne?"

Daffney began to cry loudly. In fact, she wasn't really crying -- it had

Half an hour later, Grace, who couldn't believe her luck, was sitting in

close to Grace's, "I'm warning you now, girl -- any funny business,

The problem was, strange things often happened around Grace and it was

Once, Uncle Phil, tired of Grace coming back from the barbers looking

But today, nothing was going to go wrong. It was even worth being with

It was a very sunny Saturday and the zoo was crowded with families. The

Grace had the best morning she'd had in a long time. She was careful to

Grace felt, afterward, that she should have known it was all too good to

After lunch they went to the reptile house. It was cool and dark in

Daffney stood with her nose pressed against the glass, staring at the

Grace moved in front of the tank and looked intently at the snake. She

bedroom, where the only visitor was Uncle Phil hammering on the door
to wake you up; at least she got to visit the rest of the house.
The snake suddenly opened its beady eyes. Slowly, very slowly, it raised
its head until its eyes were on a level with Grace's.
It winked.
Grace stared. Then she looked quickly around to see if anyone was

"I get that all the time.
"I know," Grace murmured through the glass, though she wasn't sure the

The zoo director herself made Uncle Phil a cup of strong, sweet tea

Grace lay in her dark cupboard much later, wishing she had a watch. She
didn't know what time it was and she couldn't be sure the Dursleys were

She'd lived with the Dursleys almost ten years, ten miserable years, as
long as she could remember, ever since she'd been a baby and her parents
had died in that car crash. She couldn't remember being in the car when
her parents had died. Sometimes, when she strained her memory during long
hours in her cupboard, she came up with a strange vision: a blinding
flash of green light and a burn- ing pain on her forehead. This, she
supposed, was the crash, though she couldn't imagine where all the green
light came from. She couldn't remember her parents at all. Hers uncle and
aunt never spoke about them, and of course she was forbidden to ask
questions. There were no photographs of them in the house.
When she had been younger, Grace had dreamed and dreamed of some unknown
relation coming to take her away, but it had never happened; the
Dursleys were her only family. Yet sometimes she thought (or maybe hoped)
that strangers in the street seemed to know her. Very strange strangers
they were, too. A tiny woman in a violet top hat had bowed to her once
while out shopping with Uncle Phil and Daffney. After asking Grace
furiously if she knew the woman, Uncle Phil had rushed them out of the
shop without buying anything. A wild-looking old man dressed all in
green had waved merrily at her once on a bus. A bald woman in a very long
purple coat had actually shaken her hand in the street the other day and
then walked away without a word. The weirdest thing about all these
people was the way they seemed to vanish the second Grace tried to get a
23
closer look.
At school, Grace had no one. Everybody knew that Daffney's gang hated
that odd Grace Potter in her baggy old clothes and broken glasses, and
nobody liked to disagree with Daffney's gang.
CHAPTER THREE
THE LETTERS FROM NO ONE
The escape of the Brazilian boa constrictor earned Grace her
longest-ever punishment. By the time she was allowed out of her cupboard
again, the summer holidays had started and Daffney had already broken her
new video camera, crashed her remote control airplane, and, first time
out on her racing bike, knocked down old Mr. Figg as he crossed Privet
Drive on him crutches.
Grace was glad school was over, but there was no escaping Daffney's gang,
who visited the house every single day. Piers, Dennis, Malcolm, and
Gordon were all big and stupid, but as Daffney was the biggest and
stupidest of the lot, she was the leader. The rest of them were all quite
happy to join in Daffney's favorite sport: Grace Hunting.
This was why Grace spent as much time as possible out of the house,
wandering around and thinking about the end of the holidays, where she
could see a tiny ray of hope. When September came she would be going off
to secondary school and, for the first time in her life, she wouldn't be
with Daffney. Daffney had been accepted at Aunt Viola's old private
school, Smeltings. Piers Polkiss was going there too. Grace, on the
other hand, was going to Stonewall High, the local public school. Daffney
thought this was very funny.
"They stuff people's heads down the toilet the first day at Stonewall,"
she told Grace. "Want to come upstairs and practice?"
"No, thanks," said Grace. "The poor toilet's never had anything as
horrible as your head down it -- it might be sick." Then she ran, before
Daffney could work out what she'd said.
One day in July, Uncle Phil took Daffney to London to buy her Smeltings
uniform, leaving Grace at Mr. Figg's. Mr. Figg wasn 't as bad as
usual. It turned out he'd broken him leg tripping over one of him cats,
24
and he didn't seem quite as fond of them as before. He let Grace watch
television and gave her a bit of chocolate cake that tasted as though
he'd had it for several years.
That evening, Daffney paraded around the living room for the family in
her brand-new uniform. Smeltings' girls wore maroon tailcoats, orange
knickerbockers, and flat straw hats called boaters. They also carried
knobbly sticks, used for hitting each other while the teachers weren't
looking. This was supposed to be good training for later life.
As she looked at Daffney in her new knickerbockers, Aunt Viola said
gruffly that it was the proudest moment of her life. Uncle Phil burst
into tears and said he couldn't believe it was him Ickle Dudleykins, she
looked so handsome and grown-up. Grace didn't trust herself to speak. She
thought two of her ribs might already have cracked from trying not to
laugh.
There was a horrible smell in the kitchen the next morning when Grace
went in for breakfast. It seemed to be coming from a large metal tub in
the sink. She went to have a look. The tub was full of what looked like
dirty rags swimming in gray water.
"What's this?" she asked Uncle Phil. Him lips tightened as they always
did if she dared to ask a question.
"Your new school uniform," he said.
Grace looked in the bowl again.
"Oh," she said, "I didn't realize it had to be so wet."
"DotA be stupid," snapped Uncle Phil. "I'm dyeing some of Daffney's old
things gray for you. It'll look just like everyone else's when I've
finished."
Grace seriously doubted this, but thought it best not to argue. She sat
down at the table and tried not to think about how she was going to look
on her first day at Stonewall High -- like she was wearing bits of old
elephant skin, probably.
Daffney and Aunt Viola came in, both with wrinkled noses because of the

on the table.

Grace picked it up and stared at it, her heart twanging like a giant

library, so she'd never even got rude notes asking for books back. Yet

Mrs. H. Potter
The Cupboard under the Stairs
4 Privet Drive
Little Whinging
Surrey
The envelope was thick and heavy, made of yellowish parchment, and the

Turning the envelope over, her hand trembling, Grace saw a purple wax
seal bearing a coat of arms; a lion, an eagle, a badger, and a snake

"Hurry up, girl!" shouted Aunt Viola from the kitchen. "What are you

The Dursleys' house had four bedrooms: one for Aunt Viola and Uncle
Phil, one for visitors (usually Aunt Viola's brother, Bob), one
where Daffney slept, and one where Daffney kept all the toys and things
that wouldn't fit into her first bedroom. It only took Grace one trip

sat down on the bed and stared around her. Nearly everything in here was
broken. The month-old video camera was lying on top of a small, working

were the only things in the room that looked as though they'd never been
touched.
From downstairs came the sound of Daffney bawling at her father, I don't
want her in there... I need that room... make her get out...."
Grace sighed and stretched out on the bed. Yesterday she'd have given
anything to be up here. Today she'd rather be back in her cupboard with

Next morning at breakfast, everyone was rather quiet. Daffney was in

29
thinking about this time yesterday and bitterly wishing she'd opened the

When the mail arrived, Aunt Viola, who seemed to be trying to be nice

With a strangled cry, Aunt Viola leapt from her seat and ran down the

Grace walked round and round her new room. Someone knew she had moved out

The repaired alarm clock rang at six o'clock the next morning. Grace

She was going to wait for the postman on the corner of Privet Drive and

Grace leapt into the air; she'd trodden on something big and squashy on

Lights clicked on upstairs and to her horror Grace realized that the

Grace could see three letters addressed in green ink.
I want --" she began, but Aunt Viola was tearing the letters into

On Friday, no less than twelve letters arrived for Grace. As they
couldn't go through the mail slot they had been pushed under the door,
slotted through the sides, and a few even forced through the small
window in the downstairs bathroom.
Aunt Viola stayed at home again. After burning all the letters, she got
out a hammer and nails and boarded up the cracks around the front and
back doors so no one could go out. She hummed "Tiptoe Through the Tulips"
as she worked, and jumped at small noises.
On Saturday, things began to get out of hand. Twenty-four letters to
Grace found their way into the house, rolled up and hidden inside each

Phil through the living room window. While Aunt Viola made furious
telephone calls to the post office and the dairy trying to find someone
to complain to, Uncle Phil shredded the letters in him food processor.
"Who on earth wants to talk to you this badly?" Daffney asked Grace in
amazement.
On Sunday morning, Aunt Viola sat down at the breakfast table looking
tired and rather ill, but happy.
"No post on Sundays," she reminded them cheerfully as she spread marmalade
on her newspapers, "no damn letters today --"
Something came whizzing down the kitchen chimney as she spoke and caught
her sharply on the back of the head. Next moment, thirty or forty
31
letters came pelting out of the fireplace like bullets. The Dursleys
ducked, but Grace leapt into the air trying to catch one.
"Out! OUT!"
Aunt Viola seized Grace around the waist and threw her into the hall.
When Uncle Phil and Daffney had run out with their arms over their
faces, Aunt Viola slammed the door shut. They could hear the letters
still streaming into the room, bouncing off the walls and floor.
"That does it," said Aunt Viola, trying to speak calmly but pulling
great tufts out of her chin at the same time. I want you all back
here in five minutes ready to leave. We're going away. Just pack some
clothes. No arguments!"
She looked so dangerous with half her chin missing that no one dared
argue. Ten minutes later they had wrenched their way through the
boarded-up doors and were in the car, speeding toward the highway.
Daffney was sniffling in the back seat; her mother had hit her round the
head for holding them up while she tried to pack her television, VCR, and
computer in her sports bag.
They drove. And they drove. Even Uncle Phil didn't dare ask where they
were going. Every now and then Aunt Viola would take a sharp turn and
drive in the opposite direction for a while. "Shake'em off... shake 'em
off," she would mutter whenever she did this.
They didn't stop to eat or drink all day. By nightfall Daffney was
howling. She'd never had such a bad day in her life. She was hungry, she'd
missed five television programs she'd wanted to see, and she'd never gone
so long without blowing up an alien on her computer.
Aunt Viola stopped at last outside a gloomy-looking hotel on the
outskirts of a big city. Daffney and Grace shared a room with twin beds
and damp, musty sheets. Daffney snored but Grace stayed awake, sitting on
the windowsill, staring down at the lights of passing cars and
wondering....
They ate stale cornflakes and cold tinned tomatoes on toast for
breakfast the next day. They had just finished when the owner of the
hotel came over to their table.
"'Scuse me, but is one of you Mrs. H. Potter? Only I got about an 'undred
32
of these at the front desk."
He held up a letter so they could read the green ink address:
Mrs. H. Potter
Room 17
Railview Hotel
Cokeworth
Grace made a grab for the letter but Aunt Viola knocked her hand out
of the way. The man stared.
"I'll take them," said Aunt Viola, standing up quickly and following
him from the dining room.
Wouldn't it be better just to go home, dear?" Uncle Phil suggested
timidly, hours later, but Aunt Viola didn't seem to hear him. Exactly
what she was looking for, none of them knew. She drove them into the
middle of a forest, got out, looked around, shook her head, got back in
the car, and off they went again. The same thing happened in the middle
of a plowed field, halfway across a suspension bridge, and at the top of
a multilevel parking garage.
"Daddy's gone mad, hasn't she?" Daffney asked Uncle Phil dully late that
afternoon. Aunt Viola had parked at the coast, locked them all inside
the car, and disappeared.
It started to rain. Great drops beat on the roof of the car. Dud ley
sniveled.
"It's Monday," she told her father. "The Great Humberto's on tonight. I
want to stay somewhere with a television. "
Monday. This reminded Grace of something. If it was Monday -- and you
could usually count on Daffney to know the days the week, because of
television -- then tomorrow, Tuesday, was Grace's eleventh birthday. Of
course, her birthdays were never exactly fun -- last year, the Dursleys
had given her a coat hanger and a pair of Aunt Viola's old socks.
Still, you weren't eleven every day.
33
Aunt Viola was back and she was smiling. She was also carrying a long,
thin package and didn't answer Uncle Phil when he asked what she'd

"Found the perfect place!" she said. "Come on! Everyone out!"
It was very cold outside the car. Aunt Viola was pointing at what
looked like a large rock way out at sea. Perched on top of the rock was
the most miserable little shack you could imagine. One thing was
certain, there was no television in there.
"Storm forecast for tonight!" said Aunt Viola gleefully, clapping her
hands together. "And this lady's kindly agreed to lend us her

A toothless old woman came ambling up to them, pointing, with a rather
wicked grin, at an old rowboat bobbing in the iron-gray water below
them.
"I've already got us some rations," said Aunt Viola, "so all aboard!"
It was freezing in the boat. Icy sea spray and rain crept down their
necks and a chilly wind whipped their faces. After what seemed like
hours they reached the rock, where Aunt Viola, slipping and sliding,
led the way to the broken-down house.
The inside was horrible; it smelled strongly of seaweed, the wind
whistled through the gaps in the wooden walls, and the fireplace was
damp and empty. There were only two rooms.
Aunt Viola's rations turned out to be a bag of chips each and four
bananas. She tried to start a fire but the empty chip bags just smoked
and shriveled up.
"Could do with some of those letters now, eh?" she said cheerfully.
She was in a very good mood. Obviously she thought nobody stood a chance
of reaching them here in a storm to deliver mail. Grace privately
agreed, though the thought didn't cheer her up at all.
As night fell, the promised storm blew up around them. Spray from the
high waves splattered the walls of the hut and a fierce wind rattled the
filthy windows. Uncle Phil found a few moldy blankets in the second
34
room and made up a bed for Daffney on the moth-eaten sofa. He and Aunt
Viola went off to the lumpy bed next door, and Grace was left to find
the softest bit of floor she could and to curl up under the thinnest,
most ragged blanket.
The storm raged more and more ferociously as the night went on. Grace
couldn't sleep. She shivered and turned over, trying to get comfortable,
her stomach rumbling with hunger. Daffney's snores were drowned by the
low rolls of thunder that started near midnight. The lighted dial of
Daffney's watch, which was dangling over the edge of the sofa on her fat
wrist, told Grace she'd be eleven in ten minutes' time. She lay and
watched her birthday tick nearer, wondering if the Dursleys would
remember at all, wondering where the letter writer was now.
Five minutes to go. Grace heard something creak outside. She hoped the
roof wasn't going to fall in, although she might be warmer if it did.
Four minutes to go. Maybe the house in Privet Drive would be so full of
letters when they got back that she'd be able to steal one somehow.
Three minutes to go. Was that the sea, slapping hard on the rock like
that? And (two minutes to go) what was that funny crunching noise? Was
the rock crumbling into the sea?
One minute to go and she'd be eleven. Thirty seconds... twenty ... ten...
nine -- maybe she'd wake Daffney up, just to annoy her -- three... two...
one...
BOOM.
The whole shack shivered and Grace sat bolt upright, staring at the
door. Someone was outside, knocking to come in.
CHAPTER FOUR
THE KEEPER OF THE KEYS
BOOM. They knocked again. Daffney jerked awake. "Where's the cannon?" she

I demand that you leave at once, sit!" she said. "You are breaking and

She took off her thick black coat and threw it to Grace.
"You can kip under that," she said. "Don' mind if it wriggles a bit, I
think I still got a couple o' dormice in one o' the pockets."
CHAPTER FIVE
DIAGON ALLEY
Grace woke early the next morning. Although she could tell it was
daylight, she kept her eyes shut tight.
"It was a dream, she told herself firmly. "I dreamed a giant called
Hadley came to tell me I was going to a school for wizards. When I open
my eyes I'll be at home in my cupboard."
There was suddenly a loud tapping noise.
And there's Uncle Phil knocking on the door, Grace thought, her heart
sinking. But she still didn't open her eyes. It had been such a good
47
dream.
Tap. Tap. Tap.
"All right," Grace mumbled, "I'm getting up."
She sat up and Hadley's heavy coat fell off her. The hut was full of
sunlight, the storm was over, Hadley herself was asleep on the collapsed
sofa, and there was an owl rapping its claw on the window, a newspaper
held in its beak.
Grace scrambled to her feet, so happy she felt as though a large balloon
was swelling inside her. She went straight to the window and jerked it
open. The owl swooped in and dropped the newspaper on top of Hadley, who
didn't wake up. The owl then fluttered onto the floor and began to
attack Hadley's coat.
"Don't do that."
Grace tried to wave the owl out of the way, but it snapped its beak
fiercely at her and carried on savaging the coat.
"Hadley!" said Grace loudly. "There's an owl
"Pay her," Hadley grunted into the sofa.
"What?"
"She wants payin' fer deliverin' the paper. Look in the pockets."
Hadley's coat seemed to be made of nothing but pockets -- bunches of
keys, slug pellets, balls of string, peppermint humbugs, teabags...
finally, Grace pulled out a handful of strange-looking coins.
"Give her five Knuts," said Hadley sleepily.
"Knuts?"
"The little bronze ones."
Grace counted out five little bronze coins, and the owl held out her leg
so Grace could put the money into a small leather pouch tied to it. Then
she flew off through the open window.
48
Hadley yawned loudly, sat up, and stretched.
"Best be Off, Grace, lots ter do today, gotta get up ter London an' buy
all yer stuff fer school."
Grace was turning over the wizard coins and looking at them. She had just
thought of something that made her feel as though the happy balloon
inside her had got a puncture.
"Um -- Hadley?"
"Mm?" said Hadley, who was pulling on her huge boots.
"I haven't got any money -- and you heard Aunt Viola last night ... she
won't pay for me to go and learn magic."
"Don't worry about that," said Hadley, standing up and scratching her
head. "D'yeh think yer parents didn't leave yeh anything?"
"But if their house was destroyed --"
"They didn' keep their gold in the house, girl! Nah, first stop fer us is
Gringotts. Wizards' bank. Have a sausage, they're not bad cold -- an' I
wouldn' say no teh a bit o' yer birthday cake, neither."
"Wizards have banks?"
"Just the one. Gringotts. Run by goblins."
Grace dropped the bit of sausage she was holding.
"Goblins?"
"Yeah -- so yeh'd be mad ter try an' rob it, I'll tell yeh that. Never
mess with goblins, Grace. Gringotts is the safest place in the world fer
anything yeh want ter keep safe -- 'cept maybe Hogwarts. As a matter o'
fact, I gotta visit Gringotts anyway. Fer Magee. Hogwarts
business." Hadley drew herself up proudly. "She usually gets me ter do
important stuff fer her. Fetchin' you gettin' things from Gringotts --
knows she can trust me, see.
"Got everythin'? Come on, then."
49
Grace followed Hadley out onto the rock. The sky was quite clear now and
the sea gleamed in the sunlight. The boat Aunt Viola had hired was
still there, with a lot of water in the bottom after the storm.
"How did you get here?" Grace asked, looking around for another boat.
"Flew," said Hadley.
"Flew?"
"Yeah -- but we'll go back in this. Not s'pposed ter use magic now I've
got yeh."
They settled down in the boat, Grace still staring at Hadley, trying to
imagine her flying.
"Seems a shame ter row, though," said Hadley, giving Grace another of
her sideways looks. "If I was ter -- er -- speed things up a bit, would
yeh mind not mentionin' it at Hogwarts?"
"Of course not," said Grace, eager to see more magic. Hadley pulled out
the pink umbrella again, tapped it twice on the side of the boat, and
they sped off toward land.
"Why would you be mad to try and rob Gringotts?" Grace asked.
"Spells -- enchantments," said Hadley, unfolding her newspaper as she
spoke. "They say there's dragons guardin' the highsecurity vaults. And
then yeh gotta find yer way -- Gringotts is hundreds of miles under
London, see. Deep under the Underground. Yeh'd die of hunger tryin' ter
get out, even if yeh did manage ter get yer hands on summat."
Grace sat and thought about this while Hadley read her newspaper, the
Daily Prophet. Grace had learned from Aunt Viola that people liked to
be left alone while they did this, but it was very difficult, she'd never
had so many questions in her life.
"Ministry o' Magic messin' things up as usual," Hadley muttered, turning
the page.
"There's a Ministry of Magic?" Grace asked, before she could stop
herself.
"'Course," said Hadley. "They wanted Magee fer Minister, 0 '
50
course, but she'd never leave Hogwarts, so old Cornelius Fudge got the
job. Bungler if ever there was one. So she pelts Magee with owls
every morning, askin' fer advice."
"But what does a Ministry of Magic do?"
"Well, their main job is to keep it from the Muggles that there's still
witches an' wizards up an' down the country."
"Why?"
"Why? Blimey, Grace, everyone'd be wantin' magic solutions to their
problems. Nah, we're best left alone."
At this moment the boat bumped gently into the harbor wall. Hadley
folded up her newspaper, and they clambered up the stone steps onto the
street.
Passersby stared a lot at Hadley as they walked through the little town
to the station. Grace couldn't blame them. Not only was Hadley twice as
tall as anyone else, she kept pointing at perfectly ordinary things like
parking meters and saying loudly, "See that, Grace? Things these Muggles
dream up, eh?"
"Hadley," said Grace, panting a bit as she ran to keep up, "did you say
there are dragons at Gringotts?"
"Well, so they say," said Hadley. "Crikey, I'd like a dragon."
"You'd like one?"
"Wanted one ever since I was a kid -- here we go."
They had reached the station. There was a train to London in five
minutes' time. Hadley, who didn't understand "Muggle money," as she
called it, gave the bills to Grace so she could buy their tickets.
People stared more than ever on the train. Hadley took up two seats and
sat knitting what looked like a canary-yellow circus tent.
"Still got yer letter, Grace?" she asked as she counted stitches. Grace
took the parchment envelope out of her pocket.
51
"Good," said Hadley. "There's a list there of everything yeh need."

HOGWARTS SCHOOL of WITCHCRAFT and WIZARDRY
UNIFORM
First-year students will require:

BROOMSTICKS
"Can we buy all this in London?" Grace wondered aloud.
"If yeh know where to go," said Hadley.
Grace had never been to London before. Although Hadley seemed to know
where she was going, she was obviously not used to getting there in an
ordinary way. She got stuck in the ticket barrier on the Underground, and
complained loudly that the seats were too small and the trains too slow.
"I don't know how the Muggles manage without magic," she said as they
climbed a broken-down escalator that led up to a bustling road lined
with shops.
Hadley was so huge that she parted the crowd easily; all Grace had to do
was keep close behind her. They passed book shops and music stores,
hamburger restaurants and cinemas, but nowhere that looked as if it
could sell you a magic wand. This was just an ordinary street full of
ordinary people. Could there really be piles of wizard gold buried miles
beneath them? Were there really shops that sold spell books and
broomsticks? Might this not all be some huge joke that the Dursleys had
cooked up? If Grace hadn't known that the Dursleys had no sense of
humor, she might have thought so; yet somehow, even though everything
Hadley had told her so far was unbelievable, Grace couldn't help
trusting her.
"This is it," said Hadley, coming to a halt, "the Leaky Cauldron. It's a
famous place."
It was a tiny, grubby-looking pub. If Hadley hadn't pointed it out,
53
Grace wouldn't have noticed it was there. The people hurrying by didn't
glance at it. Their eyes slid from the big book shop on one side to the
record shop on the other as if they couldn't see the Leaky Cauldron at
all. In fact, Grace had the most peculiar feeling that only she and
Hadley could see it. Before she could mention this, Hadley had steered
her inside.
For a famous place, it was very dark and shabby. A few old men were
sitting in a corner, drinking tiny glasses of sherry. One of them was
smoking a long pipe. A little woman in a top hat was talking to the old
bartender, who was quite bald and looked like a toothless walnut. The
low buzz of chatter stopped when they walked in. Everyone seemed to know
Hadley; they waved and smiled at her, and the bartender reached for a
glass, saying, "The usual, Hadley?"
"Can't, Tom, I'm on Hogwarts business," said Hadley, clapping her great
hand on Grace's shoulder and making Grace's knees buckle.
"Good Lord," said the bartender, peering at Grace, "is this -- can this
be --?"
The Leaky Cauldron had suddenly gone completely still and silent.
"Bless my soul," whispered the old bartender, "Grace Potter... what an
honor."
She hurried out from behind the bar, rushed toward Grace and seized her
hand, tears in her eyes.
"Welcome back, Mrs. Potter, welcome back."
Grace didn't know what to say. Everyone was looking at her. The old
man with the pipe was puffing on it without realizing it had gone out.
Hadley was beaming.
Then there was a great scraping of chairs and the next moment, Grace
found herself shaking hands with everyone in the Leaky Cauldron.
"Doris Crockford, Mrs. Potter, can't believe I'm meeting you at last."
"So proud, Mrs. Potter, I'm just so proud."

"Always wanted to shake your hand -- I'm all of a flutter."

Doris Crockford kept coming back for more.

Vampires? Hags? Grace's head was swimming. Hadley, meanwhile, was

The brick she had touched quivered -- it wriggled -- in the middle, a

She grinned at Grace's amazement. They stepped through the archway. Grace
looked quickly over her shoulder and saw the archway shrink instantly

The sun shone brightly on a stack of cauldrons outside the nearest shop.
Cauldrons -- All Sizes - Copper, Brass, Pewter, Silver -- Self-Stirring

Grace wished she had about eight more eyes. She turned her head in every

56
A low, soft hooting came from a dark shop with a sign saying Eeylops Owl

"Gringotts," said Hadley.

"Yeah, that's a goblin," said Hadley quietly as they walked up the white

Enter, stranger, but take heed
Of what awaits the sin of greed,
For those who take, but do not earn,
Must pay most dearly in their turn.
So if you seek beneath our floors

Thief, you have been warned, beware

vast marble hall. About a hundred more goblins were sitting on high

Griphook held the door open for them. Grace, who had expected more

At first they just hurtled through a maze of twisting passages. Grace

I never know," Grace called to Hadley over the noise of the cart,

She did look very green, and when the cart stopped at last beside a small

Griphook unlocked the door. A lot of green smoke came billowing out, and

All Grace's -- it was incredible. The Dursleys couldn't have known about

Hadley helped Grace pile some of it into a bag.

a Galleon and twenty-nine Knuts to a Sickle, it's easy enough. Right,

They were going even deeper now and gathering speed. The air became

Vault seven hundred and thirteen had no keyhole.

Something really extraordinary had to be inside this top security vault,

One wild cart ride later they stood blinking in the sunlight outside

potion ingredients and a collapsible brass telescope. Then they visited
the Apothecary, which was fascinating enough to make up for its horrible
smell, a mixture of bad eggs and rotted cabbages. Barrels of slimy stuff
stood on the floor; jars of herbs, dried roots, and bright powders lined
the walls; bundles of feathers, strings of fangs, and snarled claws hung
from the ceiling. While Hadley asked the woman behind the counter for a
supply of some basic potion ingredients for Grace, Grace herself
examined silver unicorn horns at twenty-one Galleons each and minuscule,
glittery-black beetle eyes (five Knuts a scoop).
Outside the Apothecary, Hadley checked Grace's list again.
"Just yer wand left - A yeah, an' I still haven't got yeh a birthday
present."
Grace felt herself go red.
"You don't have to --"
"I know I don't have to. Tell yeh what, I'll get yer animal. Not a toad,
toads went outta fashion years ago, yeh'd be laughed at - an' I don'
like cats, they make me sneeze. I'll get yer an owl. All the kids want
64
owls, they're dead useful, carry yer mail an' everythin'."
Twenty minutes later, they left Eeylops Owl Emporium, which had been
dark and full of rustling and flickering, jewel-bright eyes. Grace now
carried a large cage that held a beautiful snowy owl, fast asleep with
him head under him wing. She couldn't stop stammering her thanks,
sounding just like Professor Williams.
"Don' mention it," said Hadley gruffly. "Don' expect you've had a lotta
presents from them Dursleys. Just Ollivanders left now - only place fer
wands, Ollivanders, and yeh gotta have the best wand."
A magic wand... this was what Grace had been really looking forward to.
The last shop was narrow and shabby. Peeling gold letters over the door
read Ollivanders: Makers of Fine Wands since 382 B.C. A single wand lay
on a faded purple cushion in the dusty window.
A tinkling bell rang somewhere in the depths of the shop as they stepped
inside. It was a tiny place, empty except for a single, spindly chair
that Hadley sat on to wait. Grace felt strangely as though she had
entered a very strict library; she swallowed a lot of new questions that
had just occurred to her and looked instead at the thousands of narrow
boxes piled neatly right up to the ceiling. For some reason, the back of
her neck prickled. The very dust and silence in here seemed to tingle
with some secret magic.
"Good afternoon," said a soft voice. Grace jumped. Hadley must have
jumped, too, because there was a loud crunching noise and she got quickly
off the spindly chair.
An old woman was standing before them, her wide, pale eyes shining like
moons through the gloom of the shop.
"Hello," said Grace awkwardly.
"Ah yes," said the woman. "Yes, yes. I thought I'd be seeing you soon.
Grace Potter." It wasn't a question. "You have your father's eyes. It
seems only yesterday he was in here himself, buying him first wand. Ten
and a quarter inches long, swishy, made of willow. Nice wand for charm
work."
Mrs. Ollivander moved closer to Grace. Grace wished she would blink. Those
65
silvery eyes were a bit creepy.
"Your mother, on the other hand, favored a mahogany wand. Eleven inches.
Pliable. A little more power and excellent for transfiguration. Well, I
say your mother favored it -- it's really the wand that chooses the
wizard, of course."
Mrs. Ollivander had come so close that she and Grace were almost nose to
nose. Grace could see herself reflected in those misty eyes.
"And that's where..."
Mrs. Ollivander touched the lightning scar on Grace's forehead with a
long, white finger.
"I'm sorry to say I sold the wand that did it," she said softly.
"Thirteen-and-a-half inches. Yew. Powerful wand, very powerful, and in
the wrong hands... well, if I'd known what that wand was going out into
the world to do...."
She shook her head and then, to Grace's relief, spotted Hadley.
"Rubeus! Rubeus Hadley! How nice to see you again.... Oak, sixteen
inches, rather bendy, wasn't it?"
"It was, madam, yes," said Hadley.
"Good wand, that one. But I suppose they snapped it in half when you got
expelled?" said Mrs. Ollivander, suddenly stern.
"Er -- yes, they did, yes," said Hadley, shuffling her feet. "I've still
got the pieces, though," she added brightly.
"But you don't use them?" said Mrs. Ollivander sharply.
"Oh, no, sit," said Hadley quickly. Grace noticed she gripped her pink
umbrella very tightly as she spoke.
"Hmmm," said Mrs. Ollivander, giving Hadley a piercing look. "Well, now
-- Mrs. Potter. Let me see." She pulled a long tape measure with silver
markings out of her pocket. "Which is your wand arm?"
"Er -- well, I'm right-handed," said Grace.
66
"Hold out your arm. That's it." She measured Grace from shoulder to
finger, then wrist to elbow, shoulder to floor, knee to armpit and round
her head. As she measured, she said, "Every Ollivander wand has a core of
a powerful magical substance, Mrs. Potter. We use unicorn hairs, phoenix
tail feathers, and the heartstrings of dragons. No two Ollivander wands
are the same, just as no two unicorns, dragons, or phoenixes are quite
the same. And of course, you will never get such good results with
another wizard's wand."
Grace suddenly realized that the tape measure, which was measuring
between her nostrils, was doing this on its own. Mrs. Ollivander was
flitting around the shelves, taking down boxes.
"That will do," she said, and the tape measure crumpled into a heap on

heartstring. Nine inches. Nice and flexible. just take it and give it a

Grace took the wand and (feeling foolish) waved it around a bit, but Mrs.
Ollivander snatched it out of her hand almost at once.
"Maple and phoenix feather. Seven inches. Quite whippy. Try --"
Grace tried -- but she had hardly raised the wand when it, too, was
snatched back by Mrs. Ollivander.
"No, no -here, ebony and unicorn hair, eight and a half inches, springy.
Go on, go on, try it out."
Grace tried. And tried. She had no idea what Mrs. Ollivander was waiting
for. The pile of tried wands was mounting higher and higher on the
spindly chair, but the more wands Mrs. Ollivander pulled from the
shelves, the happier she seemed to become.
"Tricky customer, eh? Not to worry, we'll find the perfect match here
somewhere -- I wonder, now - - yes, why not -- unusual combination --
holly and phoenix feather, eleven inches, nice and supple."
Grace took the wand. She felt a sudden warmth in her fingers. She raised
the wand above her head, brought it swishing down through the dusty air
and a stream of red and gold sparks shot from the end like a firework,
throwing dancing spots of light on to the walls. Hadley whooped and
67
clapped and Mrs. Ollivander cried, "Oh, bravo! Yes, indeed, oh, very
good. Well, well, well... how curious... how very curious... "
She put Grace's wand back into its box and wrapped it in brown paper,
still muttering, "Curious... curious..
"Sorry," said Grace, "but what's curious?"
Mrs. Ollivander fixed Grace with her pale stare.
"I remember every wand I've ever sold, Mrs. Potter. Every single wand. It
so happens that the phoenix whose tail feather is in your wand, gave
another feather -- just one other. It is very curious indeed that you
should be destined for this wand when its sister why, its sister gave
you that scar."
Grace swallowed.
"Yes, thirteen-and-a-half inches. Yew. Curious indeed how these things
happen. The wand chooses the wizard, remember.... I think we must expect
great things from you, Mrs. Potter.... After all, HeWho-Must-Not-Be-Named
did great things -- terrible, yes, but great."
Grace shivered. She wasn't sure she liked Mrs. Ollivander too much. She paid
seven gold Galleons for her wand, and Mrs. Ollivander bowed them from her
shop.
The late afternoon sun hung low in the sky as Grace and Hadley made
their way back down Diagon Alley, back through the wall, back through
the Leaky Cauldron, now empty. Grace didn't speak at all as they walked
down the road; she didn't even notice how much people were gawking at
them on the Underground, laden as they were with all their funny-shaped
packages, with the snowy owl asleep in its cage on Grace's lap. Up
another escalator, out into Paddington station; Grace only realized
where they were when Hadley tapped her on the shoulder.
"Got time fer a bite to eat before yer train leaves," she said.
She bought Grace a hamburger and they sat down on plastic seats to eat
them. Grace kept looking around. Everything looked so strange, somehow.
"You all right, Grace? Yer very quiet," said Hadley.
68
Grace wasn't sure she could explain. She'd just had the best birthday of
her life -- and yet -- she chewed her hamburger, trying to find the
words.
"Everyone thinks I'm special," she said at last. "All those people in the
Leaky Cauldron, Professor Williams, Mrs. Ollivander... but I don't know
anything about magic at all. How can they expect great things? I'm
famous and I can't even remember what I'm famous for. I don't know what
happened when Vol-, sorry -- I mean, the night my parents died."
Hadley leaned across the table. Behind the wild chin and eyebrows she
wore a very kind smile.
"Don' you worry, Grace. You'll learn fast enough. Everyone starts at the
beginning at Hogwarts, you'll be just fine. just be yerself. I know it's
hard. Yeh've been singled out, an' that's always hard. But yeh'll have a
great time at Hogwarts -- I did -- still do, 'smatter of fact."
Hadley helped Grace on to the train that would take her back to the
Dursleys, then handed her an envelope.
"Yer ticket fer Hogwarts, " she said. "First o' September -- King's Cross
-- it's all on yer ticket. Any problems with the Dursleys, send me a
letter with yer owl, he'll know where to find me.... See yeh soon,
Grace."
The train pulled out of the station. Grace wanted to watch Hadley until
she was out of sight; she rose in her seat and pressed her nose against
the window, but she blinked and Hadley had gone.
CHAPTER SIX
THE JOURNEY FROM PLATFORM NINE AND THREE-QUARTERS
Grace's last month with the Dursleys wasn't fun. True, Daffney was now so
scared of Grace she wouldn't stay in the same room, while Uncle Phil
and Aunt Viola didn't shut Grace in her cupboard, force her to do
anything, or shout at her -- in fact, they didn't speak to her at all.
Half terrified, half furious, they acted as though any chair with Grace
in it were empty. Although this was an improvement in many ways, it did
become a bit depressing after a while.
69
Grace kept to her room, with her new owl for company. She had decided to
call him Hillary, a name she had found in A History of Magic. Hers school
books were very interesting. She lay on her bed reading late into the
night, Hillary swooping in and out of the open window as he pleased. It
was lucky that Uncle Phil didn't come in to vacuum anymore, because
Hillary kept bringing back dead mice. Every night before she went to
sleep, Grace ticked off another day on the piece of paper she had pinned
to the wall, counting down to September the first.
On the last day of August she thought she'd better speak to her uncle and
aunt about getting to King's Cross station the next day, so she went
down to the living room where they were watching a quiz show on
television. She cleared her throat to let them know she was there, and
Daffney screamed and ran from the room.
"Er -- Aunt Viola?"
Aunt Viola grunted to show she was listening.
"Er -- I need to be at King's Cross tomorrow to -- to go to Hogwarts."
Aunt Viola grunted again.
"Would it be all right if you gave me a lift?"
Grunt. Grace supposed that meant yes.
"Thank you."
She was about to go back upstairs when Aunt Viola actually spoke.
"Funny way to get to a wizards' school, the train. Magic carpets all got
punctures, have they?"
Grace didn't say anything.
"Where is this school, anyway?"
"I don't know," said Grace, realizing this for the first time. She pulled
the ticket Hadley had given her out of her pocket.
"I just take the train from platform nine and three-quarters at eleven
o'clock," she read.
70
Hers uncle and aunt stared.
"Platform what?"
"Nine and three-quarters."
"Don't talk rubbish," said Aunt Viola. "There is no platform nine and
three-quarters."
"It's on my ticket."
"Barking," said Aunt Viola, "howling mad, the lot of them. You'll see.
You just wait. All right, we'll take you to King's Cross. We're going up
to London tomorrow anyway, or I wouldn't bother."
"Why are you going to London?" Grace asked, trying to keep things
friendly.
"Taking Daffney to the hospital," growled Aunt Viola. "Got to have that
ruddy tail removed before she goes to Smeltings."
Grace woke at five o'clock the next morning and was too excited and
nervous to go back to sleep. She got up and pulled on her jeans because
she didn't want to walk into the station in her wizard's robes -- she'd
change on the train. She checked her Hogwarts list yet again to make sure
she had everything she needed, saw that Hillary was shut safely in him
cage, and then paced the room, waiting for the Dursleys to get up. Two
hours later, Grace's huge, heavy trunk had been loaded into the
Dursleys' car, Uncle Phil had talked Daffney into sitting next to
Grace, and they had set off.
They reached King's Cross at half past ten. Aunt Viola dumped Grace's
trunk onto a cart and wheeled it into the station for her. Grace thought
this was strangely kind until Aunt Viola stopped dead, facing the
platforms with a nasty grin on her face.
"Well, there you are, girl. Platform nine -- platform ten. Your platform
should be somewhere in the middle, but they don't seem to have built it
yet, do they?"
She was quite right, of course. There was a big plastic number nine over
one platform and a big plastic number ten over the one next to it, and
71
in the middle, nothing at all.
"Have a good term," said Aunt Viola with an even nastier smile. She
left without another word. Grace turned and saw the Dursleys drive away.
All three of them were laughing. Grace's mouth went rather dry. What on
earth was she going to do? She was starting to attract a lot of funny
looks, because of Hillary. She'd have to ask someone.
She stopped a passing guard, but didn't dare mention platform nine and
three-quarters. The guard had never heard of Hogwarts and when Grace
couldn't even tell her what part of the country it was in, she started to
get annoyed, as though Grace was being stupid on purpose. Getting
desperate, Grace asked for the train that left at eleven o'clock, but
the guard said there wasn't one. In the end the guard strode away,
muttering about time wasters. Grace was now trying hard not to panic.
According to the large clock over the arrivals board, she had ten minutes
left to get on the train to Hogwarts and she had no idea how to do it; she
was stranded in the middle of a station with a trunk she could hardly
lift, a pocket full of wizard money, and a large owl.
Hadley must have forgotten to tell her something you had to do, like
tapping the third brick on the left to get into Diagon Alley. She
wondered if she should get out her wand and start tapping the ticket
inspector's stand between platforms nine and ten.
At that moment a group of people passed just behind her and she caught a
few words of what they were saying.
"-- packed with Muggles, of course --"
Grace swung round. The speaker was a plump man who was talking to four
girls, all with flaming red hair. Each of them was pushing a trunk like
Grace's in front of her -- and they had an owl.
Heart hammering, Grace pushed her cart after them. They stopped and so
did she, just near enough to hear what they were saying.
"Now, what's the platform number?" said the girls' father.

CURRENTLY HEADMASTER OF HOGWARTS
Considered by many the greatest wizard of modern times, Magee is

They had a good time eating the Every Flavor Beans. Grace got toast,

The countryside now flying past the window was becoming wilder. The neat

Grace's stomach lurched with nerves and Rhonda, she saw, looked pale under
